      "source": [
        "You can embed words in two ways:\n",
        "\n",
        "* They can be taught along with the main task (e.g. classifying the document or predicting sentiment). In this case, the work begins with random vectors of words, and then the content of these vectors is learned in the same way as the weights of the neural network.\n",
        "* You can load word embeds created while working on another machine learning problem into the model. Such embeddings are referred to as pre-trained word embeddings.\n",
        "  \n",
        "Let's look at both solutions."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "N2zl4d0ezsSp"
      },
      "source": [
        "## Teach word embedding using the embedding layer\n",
        "\n",
        " The easiest way to associate a dense vector with a word is to choose a random vector. The problem with this solution is that the resulting space of depositions has no structure – for example, the words beautiful and cute can end up in completely different embeddings, despite the fact that in most sentences they are synonyms. A neural network may have trouble organizing such a noisy and unstructured deposit space.\n",
        "\n",
        "Let's move on to the level of an abstraction. The geometric relationship between word vectors should reflect the semantic relationship between these words. Word embeddings are meant to reflect language in geometric space. In the correct settlement space, synonyms should be embedded in similar word vectors. In general, the geometric distance (e.g. distance L2) between any two words should reflect the semantic difference between them. In the space of depositions, not only distance matters – directions should also have a certain meaning. Let's explain it with a specific example.\n",
        "\n",
        "[...]\n",
        "\n",
        "In fact, embedding spaces allow you to perform gender-changing transformations or get plural results. By adding the vector of the \"feminine gender\" to the vector \"king\", we get the vector \"queen\". By adding the \"plural\" vector to the \"king\" vector, we get the \"kings\" vector. Word embedding spaces typically contain thousands of vectors that can be interpreted and potentially used.\n",
        "\n",
        "Is there a perfect space for embedding words that would  reflect the relationships between the words used by people? Perhaps, but no one has obtained it yet. There is no such thing as human language — people speak many different languages that are not isometric (language is a reflection of culture and a specific context). In practice, whether a given space is good depends mainly on the problem we want to solve. The ideal space for the embedding of English words in the context of the sentiment analysis model for film reviews looks different from the ideal space of embedding  English words for the model classifying legal documents. This is due to the fact that in each of these problems different semantic dependencies are important.\n",
        "\n",
        "Therefore, it is worth training a new embedding space while solving a new problem. Fortunately, this process is facilitated by the reverse propagation algorithm and the Keras package. It all comes down to learning the weight value of the Embedding embedding layer."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 2,
      "metadata": {
        "collapsed": true,
        "id": "l2zVVEOMzsSq"
      },
      "outputs": [],
      "source": [
        "from keras.layers import Embedding\n",
        "\n",
        "# The Embedding layer takes at least two arguments: \n",
        "# number of tokens (here 1000: 1 + maximum word index) \n",
        "# and the number of settlement dimensions (here 64).\n",
        "embedding_layer = Embedding(1000, 64)"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "rlX_OyWlzsSq"
      },
      "source": [
        "The Embedding layer is best understood as a dictionary that maps integer indexes that denote specific words to dense vectors. It takes integer values at the input and searches for them in the internal dictionary, returning the vectors that are associated. Figure 6.4 shows a diagram of the dictionary search operations."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "7ppx4VPHzsSr"
      },
      "source": [
        "The Embedding layer adopts a two-dimensional tensor of a shape (samples, sequence_length) at the input. Each element of this tensor is a sequence of integers. This layer can embed sequences of variable length: the Embedding layer shown in the previous example can be targeted with a shape (32, 10) (32 sequences of length of 10) or batches of shape (64, 15) (batch 64 sequences of length of 15). All sequences included in the batch must be of the same length (this is due to the fact that they are placed in the same tensor), so sequences that are shorter than others should be completed with zeros, and sequences that are longer should be cut.\n",
        "\n",
        "This layer returns a three-dimensional floating-point tensor of shape (samples, , sequence_length, inclusion_layer_number). Such a three-dimensional tensor can be processed by the RNN layer or a one-dimensional convolution layer (both solutions will be described in the following sections).\n",
        "\n",
        "When you create an instance of an Embedding layer, its weights (the internal token vector dictionary) are randomly initiated (as is the case with any other layer). During training, word vectors are gradually fine-tuned using a backward propagation algorithm, leading to a space that the model can use. Once fully trained, the embedding space will have the character of a structure specialized in solving the problem for which the model is trained.\n",
        "\n",
        "Let's use this solution in a model that predicts the sentiment of a film review included in the IMDB collection (we have tried to face this task before). Let's start with a quick data preparation. We will limit the content of reviews to the 10,000 most common words  (we also used this procedure during the first approach to this problem), and then we will cut the reviews after only 20 words. The network will learn the eight-dimensional embeddings of each of the 10,000 words, convert the input sequence of integer values (a two-dimensional integer tensor) into embedded sequences (a three-dimensional floating-point tensor), flatten this tensor to two dimensions, and train a single Dense layer located at the end of the classifier."
      ]
    },

        "from keras.datasets import imdb\n",
        "from keras import preprocessing\n",
        "\n",
        "# Number of words analyzed as weights.\n",
        "max_features = 10000\n",
        "# Cuts reviews where this word count occurs \n",
        "# (words included in the set of max_features - the most common words).\n",
        "maxlen = 20\n",
        "\n",
        "# Loads data in the form of lists of integer values.\n",
        "(x_train, y_train), (x_test, y_test) = imdb.load_data(num_words=max_features)\n",
        "\n",
        "# We replace integer lists with a two-dimensional tensor \n",
        "# integer values of shape (samples, maxlen).\n",
        "x_train = preprocessing.sequence.pad_sequences(x_train, maxlen=maxlen)\n",
        "x_test = preprocessing.sequence.pad_sequences(x_test, maxlen=maxlen)"
      ]

        "from keras.models import Sequential\n",
        "from keras.layers import Flatten, Dense\n",
        "\n",
        "model = Sequential()\n",
        "# We specify the maximum length of the input data of the Embedding layer,\n",
        "# which will allow you to flatten the embedded input later.\n",
        "model.add(Embedding(10000, 8, input_length=maxlen))\n",
        "# After processing by the deposition layer, the activations have a shape (samples, maxlen, 8).\n",
        "\n",
        "# Flattening the three-dimensional sediment tensor \n",
        "# to obtain a two-dimensional tensor of shape (samples, maxlen * 8).\n",
        "model.add(Flatten())\n",
        "\n",
        "# Add the last classifier layer.\n",
        "model.add(Dense(1, activation='sigmoid'))\n",
        "model.compile(optimizer='rmsprop', loss='binary_crossentropy', metrics=['acc'])\n",
        "model.summary()\n",
        "\n",
        "history = model.fit(x_train, y_train,\n",
        "                    epochs=10,\n",
        "                    batch_size=32,\n",
        "                    validation_split=0.2)"
      ]

      },
      "source": [
        "During validation, we obtain an accuracy of ~76%. This is a fairly good result, considering that we only analyze the first 20 words of each review. Note that flattening the embedded sequences and training one upper Layer of Dense leads to a model that treats each word of the input sequence independently. The model does not analyze the relationship between words and sentence structure (it will treat,  the sentences: \"this movie is a bomb\" and \"this movie is the bomb\" as belonging to negative reviews). A much better solution is to add recursive layers or one-dimensional convolutional layers to the embedded sequence — this will allow you to learn the features that take into account the whole sequence. I will describe these solutions in the following sections."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "RT2bESqSzsSu"
      },
      "source": [
        "## Using previously trained word embeddings\n",
        "\n",
        "Sometimes you have so little data available that it's impossible to use that data alone. It beocmes impossible to train to embed the words that are right for the problem. What can be done in such a situation?\n",
        "\n",
        "Instead of training word embedding together with the problem you're solving, you can load embedding vectors from a pre-created embedding space that is known to have the right structure and properties to address the general aspects of the language structure. The sense of using pre-trained word embeddings in natural language processing is the same as the sense of using previously trained convolutional neural networks when classifying images — if we don't have enough data for the model to train practical features on its own, and the features we expect to train are general, then for visual and semantic features we can use features learned by models working on other problems.\n",
        "\n",
        "This type of word embedding is usually created using word occurrence statistics (observation of what words appear side by side in sentences or documents). This is done using many techniques. Some of them are based on neural networks. The idea of creating a dense low-dimensional embedding word space created in an unsupervised way was initially analyzed by Bengio and others in the early 2000s, but gained popularity in practical applications only after the publication of the most famous word embedding algorithm — the Word2vec algorithm (https://code.google.com/archive/p/word2vec). This algorithm was developed in 2013 by the Google employee Tomas Mikolov. The dimensions of the Word2vec algorithm reflect specific semantic properties, such as a type (gender).\n",
        "\n",
        "There are many ready-made word embedding databases that you can download and import into the Embedding layer of Keras. One of such solutions is Word2vec. Another popular project is Global Vectors for Word Representation (GloVe): https://nlp.stanford.edu/projects/glove. This project was developed in 2014 by researchers at Stanford University. This deposition technique uses factorization of a matrix of statistical values that determine the common occurrence of words. Its authors have made available ready-made embeddings of millions of English words based on data from Wikipedia and Common Crawl.\n",
        "\n",
        "Let's look at the use of GloVe depositions in Keras models. In the same way, you can use Word2vec embeddings and other word embedding databases. While working on this example, we will also recall the text tokenization techniques described earlier – we will start with raw text."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "txOhylSvzsSv"
      },
      "source": [
        "## Combining all techniques: from raw text to word embedding\n",
        "\n",
        "We'll be using a model similar to the one we just described—we'll swap sentences for vector sequences, flatten them, and train the top Layer of Dense of our model. This time, however, we will use ready-made word embeddings. Instead of using tokenized IMDB data included with Keras, we'll start with the ground up — we'll download the data as raw text."
      ]
    },

        "import os\n",
        "\n",
        "imdb_dir = 'aclImdb'\n",
        "train_dir = os.path.join(imdb_dir, 'train')\n",
        "\n",
        "labels = []\n",
        "texts = []\n",
        "\n",
        "for label_type in ['neg', 'pos']:\n",
        "    dir_name = os.path.join(train_dir, label_type)\n",
        "    for fname in os.listdir(dir_name):\n",
        "        if fname[-4:] == '.txt':\n",
        "            f = open(os.path.join(dir_name, fname))\n",
        "            texts.append(f.read())\n",
        "            f.close()\n",
        "            if label_type == 'neg':\n",
        "                labels.append(0)\n",
        "            else:\n",
        "                labels.append(1)"
      ]

      "source": [
        "### Data tokenization\n",
        "\n",
        "Let's convert text data into vectors and prepare a training set and a validation set, using the concepts presented earlier. Previously trained word embeddings are especially useful when working when you have access to a small amount of training data (otherwise embeddings optimized for a given problem will work much better). Let's limit the training data set to the first 200 samples. We will train the video review classifier on just 200 examples.\n"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 8,
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "PE8_RrDEzsSx",
        "outputId": "4b375957-36e8-4de1-9d86-e73787191e1d"
      },
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "Found 88582 unique tokens.\n",
            "Data tensor shape: (25000, 100)\n",
            "Label tensor shape: (25000,)\n"
          ]
        }
      ],
      "source": [
        "from keras.preprocessing.text import Tokenizer\n",
        "from keras.preprocessing.sequence import pad_sequences\n",
        "import numpy as np\n",
        "\n",
        "maxlen = 100  # Shortens the review to 100 words.\n",
        "training_samples = 200  # Training on 200 samples.\n",
        "validation_samples = 10000  # Validation per 10,000 samples.\n",
        "max_words = 10000  # We take into account only the 10,000 words most common in the set.\n",
        "\n",
        "tokenizer = Tokenizer(num_words=max_words)\n",
        "tokenizer.fit_on_texts(texts)\n",
        "sequences = tokenizer.texts_to_sequences(texts)\n",
        "\n",
        "word_index = tokenizer.word_index\n",
        "print('Found %s unique tokens.' % len(word_index))\n",
        "\n",
        "data = pad_sequences(sequences, maxlen=maxlen)\n",
        "\n",
        "labels = np.asarray(labels)\n",
        "print('Data tensor shape:', data.shape)\n",
        "print('Label tensor shape:', labels.shape)\n",
        "\n",
        "\n",
        "# Divides the data into a training set and a validation set,\n",
        " # but first the data is set in random order\n",
        "  # (currently, samples are set in order from negative reviews to positive reviews).\n",
        "indices = np.arange(data.shape[0])\n",
        "np.random.shuffle(indices)\n",
        "data = data[indices]\n",
        "labels = labels[indices]\n",
        "\n",
        "x_train = data[:training_samples]\n",
        "y_train = labels[:training_samples]\n",
        "x_val = data[training_samples: training_samples + validation_samples]\n",
        "y_val = labels[training_samples: training_samples + validation_samples]"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "3ucE0JkezsSx"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Getting GloVe word embeddings\n",

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "0bv5FNCnzsSx"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Pre-processing of deposits\n",
        "\n",
        "Let's parse an unzipped text file to build an index that assigns words (in the form of strings) to vector representations (vectors of numbers)."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 10,
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "2Bm0h3H5zsSy",
        "outputId": "cc53bc5f-293a-4949-97ca-9371bd838699"
      },
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "Found 400000 word vector.\n"
          ]
        }
      ],
      "source": [
        "embeddings_index = {}\n",
        "f = open('glove.6B.100d.txt')\n",
        "for line in f:\n",
        "    values = line.split()\n",
        "    word = values[0]\n",
        "    coefs = np.asarray(values[1:], dtype='float32')\n",
        "    embeddings_index[word] = coefs\n",
        "f.close()\n",
        "\n",
        "print('Found %s word vector.' % len(embeddings_index))"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "CZ7V_mEqzsSy"
      },
      "source": [
        "Next, we will build a deposit matrix that can be loaded into the Embedding layer. This matrix must have a shape (max_words, embedding_dim), and the element must contain a embedding_dim-dimensional vector for a word with an index and a built during tokenization. We will note that index 0 is not supposed to correspond to any word or token — it is simply a space filler."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 11,
      "metadata": {
        "collapsed": true,
        "id": "H1a0SLfhzsSy"
      },
      "outputs": [],
      "source": [
        "embedding_dim = 100\n",
        "\n",
        "embedding_matrix = np.zeros((max_words, embedding_dim))\n",
        "for word, i in word_index.items():\n",
        "    embedding_vector = embeddings_index.get(word)\n",
        "    if i < max_words:\n",
        "        if embedding_vector is not None:\n",
        "            # Words not found in the embedded index will be replaced with zeros.\n",
        "            embedding_matrix[i] = embedding_vector"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "1iGIsHYtzsSz"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Define a model\n",
        "\n",
        "We will use the same architecture as in the previous model.:"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 12,
      "metadata": {
        "colab": {
          "base_uri": "https://localhost:8080/"
        },
        "id": "2SM81bt4zsSz",
        "outputId": "f86b521e-755d-4dfa-85bb-07fd7a7e03ce"
      },
      "outputs": [
        {
          "output_type": "stream",
          "name": "stdout",
          "text": [
            "Model: \"sequential_1\"\n",
            "_________________________________________________________________\n",
            " Layer (type)                Output Shape              Param #   \n",
            "=================================================================\n",
            " embedding_2 (Embedding)     (None, 100, 100)          1000000   \n",
            "                                                                 \n",
            " flatten_1 (Flatten)         (None, 10000)             0         \n",
            "                                                                 \n",
            " dense_1 (Dense)             (None, 32)                320032    \n",
            "                                                                 \n",
            " dense_2 (Dense)             (None, 1)                 33        \n",
            "                                                                 \n",
            "=================================================================\n",
            "Total params: 1,320,065\n",
            "Trainable params: 1,320,065\n",
            "Non-trainable params: 0\n",
            "_________________________________________________________________\n"
          ]
        }
      ],
      "source": [
        "from keras.models import Sequential\n",
        "from keras.layers import Embedding, Flatten, Dense\n",
        "\n",
        "model = Sequential()\n",
        "model.add(Embedding(max_words, embedding_dim, input_length=maxlen))\n",
        "model.add(Flatten())\n",
        "model.add(Dense(32, activation='relu'))\n",
        "model.add(Dense(1, activation='sigmoid'))\n",
        "model.summary()"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "KO8IdVfmzsSz"
      },
      "source": [
        "### Loading GloVe embeds into the model\n",
        "\n",
        "The Embedding layer has a single weight matrix: a two-dimensional matrix of floating-point values, in which each element of i is a word vector associated with index i. We simply need to load the prepared GloVe matrix into the Embedding layer, which is the first layer of the model:"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "code",
      "execution_count": 13,
      "metadata": {
        "collapsed": true,
        "id": "DbGhcWrbzsS0"
      },
      "outputs": [],
      "source": [
        "model.layers[0].set_weights([embedding_matrix])\n",
        "model.layers[0].trainable = False"
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "uCxRHPmszsS0"
      },
      "source": [
        "In addition, we will freeze the Embedding layer (we will assign the value False to its trainable attribute). We have already explained the reasonableness of this solution when using previously trained features of convolutional neural networks – when some elements of the model (such as the Embedding layer) have been trained earlier, and other elements (such as the classifier) are initiated by random numbers, there should be no modification of previously trained elements of the model, because this will lead to the disappearance of the knowledge stored. Large gradient update values caused by the random nature of some elements of the model will result in large changes in the trained features."
      ]
    },
    {
      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "sQSBnAGRzsS1"
      },
      "source": [

        "plt.figure()\n",
        "\n",
        "plt.plot(epochs, loss, 'bo', label='Loss of training')\n",
        "plt.plot(epochs, val_loss, 'b', label='Loss of validation')\n",
        "plt.title('Loss of training and validation')\n",
        "plt.legend()\n",
        "\n",
        "plt.show()"
      ]
    },

      "cell_type": "markdown",
      "metadata": {
        "id": "s_bGW3-MzsS3"
      },
      "source": [
        "The model quickly begins to over-fit, which is not surprising with such a small number of samples included in the training data set. For the same reason, validation accuracy is characterized by high variability, but it seems to peak at a level exceeding 50%. With such a small number of samples included in the training dataset, the characteristics of the model largely depend on which 200 samples will be selected for the training data set (these samples are selected at random). If your model achieves clearly worse parameters, try to draw another set of 200 samples (this is just an exercise, when working on a real problem you do not have the opportunity to choose training data).\n",
        "\n",
        "We can also train the same model without loading trained word depositions and without freezing the deposition layer. In this case, the model will train input token embedding that is specific to our problem. This solution, in general, works much better than using ready-made word embeddings for problems where you have an extensive set of data. We have a collection of only 200 training samples, but let's try this solution anyway."
      ]

        "from keras.models import Sequential\n",
        "from keras.layers import Embedding, Flatten, Dense\n",
        "\n",
        "model = Sequential()\n",
        "model.add(Embedding(max_words, embedding_dim, input_length=maxlen))\n",
        "model.add(Flatten())\n",
        "model.add(Dense(32, activation='relu'))\n",
        "model.add(Dense(1, activation='sigmoid'))\n",
        "model.summary()\n",
        "\n",
        "model.compile(optimizer='rmsprop',\n",
        "              loss='binary_crossentropy',\n",
        "              metrics=['acc'])\n",
        "history = model.fit(x_train, y_train,\n",
        "                    epochs=10,\n",
        "                    batch_size=32,\n",
        "                    validation_data=(x_val, y_val))"

        "Validation accuracy is maintained at a level close to 50%. In this case, the use of ready-made word embeddings allowed for better results. If we increase the number of samples included in the training data set, we will quickly get better performance than the one we obtained using ready-made word embeddings. Try to do it yourself as part of the exercises. Finally, we can check the performance of the model while processing test data. For this we need to convert the test data into tokens:"
      ]

      "source": [
        "test_dir = os.path.join(imdb_dir, 'test')\n",
        "\n",
        "labels = []\n",
        "texts = []\n",
        "\n",
        "for label_type in ['neg', 'pos']:\n",
        "    dir_name = os.path.join(test_dir, label_type)\n",
        "    for fname in sorted(os.listdir(dir_name)):\n",
        "        if fname[-4:] == '.txt':\n",
        "            f = open(os.path.join(dir_name, fname))\n",
        "            texts.append(f.read())\n",
        "            f.close()\n",
        "            if label_type == 'neg':\n",
        "                labels.append(0)\n",
        "            else:\n",
        "                labels.append(1)\n",
        "\n",
        "sequences = tokenizer.texts_to_sequences(texts)\n",
        "x_test = pad_sequences(sequences, maxlen=maxlen)\n",
        "y_test = np.asarray(labels)"
      ]
    },
